32.   ABC delegated all subproject appraisal and supervision responsibilities to its
Jiangsu and Shanghai branches. The subprojects in Jiangsu ABC were in various cities in
southern Jiangsu, and the subprojects in Shanghai ABC were on Chongming Island. Both
branches were inadequate in their portfolio management and paid very little attention to
subloan collection. Its loan collection of the Spark subprojects at the end of 1997 was
close to zero.
33.   During project implementation, the Bank Group was unable to obtain meaningful
financial information, such as profitability, arrears, and collection data from the ABC
branches. Thus, the Bank Group was not able to assess financial performances of the
ABC branches. ABC did not provide the Bank Group with its 1997 financial statements
and 1994-96 financial data were extracted from their published annual reports only. Thus
the Bank Group was not able to confirm accuracy of these data.
34.  In 1995, ABC was reclassified from a specialized bank to a commercial bank.
ABC and the Bank Group are currently preparing an ABC Commercialization Project
aiming to accelerate its transformation into a profit-oriented, modem commercial bank in
both operations and management. In light of the Spark project experience, both China and
the Bank Group should review carefully the Bank Group's assistance strategy to ABC.
35.   CIB delegated all subproject appraisal and supervision to its Jiangsu branch,
which performed adequately. At the end of 1997, CIB's current ratio remained very low



-17-
at less than 0.5 percent-the lowest since 1990, its total debts were 25 percent of equity
-the highest since 1990, and net income was only 0.4 percent of total assets-a slight
improvement from previous years. All these financial indicators implied that CIB's
financial position was extremely weak.
36.   The Jilin Provincial Trust and Investment Corporation (JPTIC) was merged
with the Jilin Province Economic Development Investment Corporation (JEDIC) in
March 1991. JPTIC had assumed the rights and obligations of JEDIC and is the financial
intermnediary channeling the World Bank Spark project funds to Jilin Spark enterprises.
The new operating license was approved by the People's Bank of China in November
1991. Essentially there were no changes in its policy statement and operations.
37.   Its current staff totals 115, of which about 25 percent are financial and economic
staff. JPTIC has a Spark Project Department whose function includes appraisal, approval
and supervision of all Spark subprojects. The Spark Department has been staffed
sufficiently during the Spark project implementation period. JPTIC's activities include
deposit taking (local and foreign currencies), trust and investment (in industrial
enterprises and real estate), leasing, securities and brokerage services and underwriting,
credit information services, and consulting services to enterprises. JPTIC remains very
closely affiliated with the Jilin provincial government and is still supporting provincial
development projects. It is the princilpal window in Jilin province dealing with foreign
currency operations.
38.   JPTIC has been marginally profitable in the last few years, yet its financial
position weakened in 1997, due to the general economic conditions in China. At the end
of December 1997, its current ratio was 1.3, declining from 1996, but better than its early
years from 1990-95. Its total debt was 40 percent of total equity and had been at this level
for many years. In 1997, its net income was only 1.2 percent of total assets. These
financial ratios indicated that JPTIC vas financially weak and is not sustainable in the
long run if among others its loan collection does not improve.
D. PROJE CT SUSTAINABILITY
39.   It is likely that project objectives are sustainable for all technical assistance
components, but uncertain for thte enterprise modernization component. The
achievements generated in all technical assistance components have already exceeded
appraisal expectations and were demonstrated by the extensive use of various project
products at the national and lower levels of the Spark prograrn and by rural enterprises,
technical colleges, and the Spark training centers. The four training centers which the
project helped financed evolved into Icey training centers in the Spark training system.
The courses they offered are in high demand and are always completely filled. Project
results had reached far beyond the thr-e project areas. The MOST and ISTIC have also
determined to disseminate as much as possible project products to rural enterprises in the
interior provinces.



- 18-
40.   During project implementation, there was a gradual change in the Spark program
from provisions of preferential policies and funding to rural enterprises to provisions of
technology, information, and management services to rural enterprises. At the same time,
the focus of the Spark program has moved from project-oriented financial and technical
assistance to Spark intensive areas and Spark small areas development. This project has
played a significant catalytic role in this change.
41.   The Spark project operated in an environment that inhibits financial institutions
from playing their role as banks. Their emphasis was and still is on lending operation, and
project supervision is not a priority. They could rarely take legal actions against those
enterprises that did not repay their loans, nor could they collect from their guarantors.
They were not allowed to provide sufficient bad debt provisions, nor could they write off
their aging outstanding loans. Enterprises were also accustomed to a culture that loan
repayment is secondary to social obligations and new investment. At the same time, there
has been mounting pressure recently towards mergers/acquisitions that may not be
advantageous to successful rural enterprises. Thus, unless there are substantial and rapid
financial and enterprise reforms in China, it would be difficult to expect subloan
repayments to improve. Thus, as far as the enterprise modernization component is
concerned, the outcome is uncertain.
E. BANK GROUP PERFORMANCE
42.   The project preparation and appraisal missions did a comprehensive review of
technical assistance requirements to improve the national Spark program for the benefit
of rural enterprises. The Bank Group contributed enormous resources in preparation for
the technical assistance components with a well-defined program that properly addressed
the most critical areas to be attended to in the first project. However, expectations of the
Bank Group on financial institutions were very high for them to deliver the commercial
credits subcomponent satisfactorily despite the rudimentary nature of financial and
economic reforms in China in the late 1980s. Less than 0.2 percent of project funds were
allocated to training of PFI staff in project appraisal and supervision. Nonetheless, even a
much larger training program for the PFIs would not have improved subloan repayment
status and subproject performances given the macroeconomic framework.
43.   After project effectiveness, the Bank Group provided continual advice to project
entities on Bank Group policies and procedures, reviews of procurement document,
particularly on procurement of consultant services, and disbursement requirements. The
Bank Group also provided advice on preparation of subproject appraisal reports and
portfolio management. Bank Group staff followed closely the progress of all project
components and provided support to minimize delays. Nonetheless, Bank Group
supervision activities on the project could have been more effective had additional staff
resources been allocated. As the borrowers had always welcomed the Bank Group's
advice and comments and had the Bank Group allocated more budget for technical
support for supervision activities, the Bank Group could have contributed more to the
project's development.



- 19-
F. BORROWER PERFORMANCE
44.   The SPO in MOST and provincial/municipal STBs in the three project areas
demonstrated full commitment to the project. The Spark Program Office, in charge of the
entire project, did an excellent job in managing implementation of all the technical
assistance components and coordinating work among all participating agencies, including
ISTIC, local ISTIs, local STBs, financial institutions and training centers. Each
province/municipality also formed a World Bank Spark Project office which managed
and oversaw implementation of their respective allocations. From 1992 to 1997, the SPO
organized seven Spark Project Implementation Workshops in the three project areas for
all project entities to review  work progress, discuss work programs, evaluate
achievements, and determine immediate and longer term goals. These workshops greatly
facilitated project implementation andl kept all participating agencies aware of project
development. The SPO also published project progress reports to be circulated among all
project entities on implementation of all components. These reports, sometimes published
as frequently as every quarter, provided informative news on progress and performances
of different agencies. Even though there were more than thirty project entities (excluding
the Spark subprojects) in the three project areas, Bank Group missions noted that the
project had been very well managed, and there was genuine cooperation among all
project entities. Even though there were many managerial changes in MOST and SPO
over the six implementation years, due to this extremely well-organized setup, project
issues have generally been resolved quickly in the past six years.
G. ASSESSMENT OF OUTCOME
45.   Overall, project results are mixed. Technical assistance components were highly
satisfactory for several reasons: (a) the Government completed all technical assistance
components with great efficiency; (b) these activities provided essential support to the
TVE sector which had exhibited extraordinary growth in the past decade; and (c) the
national Spark program was strengthened as a result. Whereas, results of the enterprise
modernization component was unsatisfactory. As most subborrowers did not repay their
loans, the PFIs and ultimately the provincial/municipal finance bureaus would have to
bear the financial cost. Despite some Bank Group subprojects demonstrated diffusion
activities of technology and managemLent know-how to nonproject TVE beneficiaries
nationwide, these benefits in terms of income and employment generated in other
enterprises from the diffusion mechanism, may or may not recover the financial cost that
provincial/municipal governments have to bear eventually.
46.   With the support of local STBs, many of the Spark subprojects became pillar
industries of their respective "Spark intensive areas." They provided not only direct and
indirect employment opportunities, but led to growth of vertical and horizontal
integration of industries in the localities.
47.   The technical assistance components had extensive direct and indirect effects on
the overall provision of technical suppcrt services by different levels of STBs to TVEs.



- 20 -
The Chongming Training Center, despite its geographic disadvantage, has evolved into
an intensive high quality training center in the Shanghai area and its utilization rate has
reached 100 percent for more than two years.
H. FUTURE OPERATION
48.   The project has laid a good foundation for the national Spark program to grow and
expand. In the short and medium term, the Spark program should concentrate on
dissemination of various project activities, such as continual development of consultant
services for rural enterprises, further improvement and promotion of instructional
packages, utilization/promotion of the two technology evaluation manuals, extensive
utilization of all training programs and skills learned in the Spark training program,
cultivating productive use of the internet, improved utilization of the MIS, and many
other activities that deserve further cultivation and dissemination, to the interior
provinces in particular. In the longer term, the Spark program should reevaluate: (a) its
role as government agency to foster the development of a rural enterprise sector which is
the least regulated and the most robust in Chinese industries; and (b) its strategy in the
development of rural enterprises in the interior provinces.
49.   In regards to the subprojects operating profitably, the Government should avoid
burdening them with asset acquisitions/mergers which may be financially unattractive to
them. As to those subprojects with operational and financial difficulties, certain actions
need to be taken by the local government, the local STBs, and the financial institutions to
help the subborrowers turn around to be operationally competitive, financially viable, and
environmentally sound over time.
X. KEY LESSONS LEARNED
50.   At the time the project was appraised, it was expected that it would be the first of
a series of subsequent operations to support the rural nonstate industrial sector. However,
as a result of the experiences gained from this project and other industrial projects and
lines of credit operations in China, the Government and the Bank Group realized that the
generic issues in the financial and enterprise sectors needed to be resolved before
committing to further lending operations to enterprises through financial institutions. This
means that subsequent operations have been significantly curtailed.
51.   The following major lessons were learned under the project:
(a) Successful execution of technical assistance requires full commitment and
participation of all stakeholders throughout the process. It was achieved in
this project. It shows that our client agencies are very receptive to technical
assistance which has proved to be very useful to the Spark program.
(b) National ownership is important. Spark is a national program and the Bank
Group is only helping to strengthen it. The Ministry of Science and
Technology takes prides in the Spark accomplishments.



- 21 -
(c) Sound organization setup and dynamic leadership for implementing the
project are essential to project success. This is especially essential for projects
that have many implementing agencies in different project areas.
(d) Provision of technical assistance needs to be driven by demand, rather than
supply. Measures and incentives to promote demand rather than promote
supply would made technical assistance more effective.
(e) Long-term (over one year) consultant contract should have the flexibility of
adjustment with the changing environment in the country.
(f) Rapid dissemination of project products should be carried out by MOST
immediately to avoid being outdated, and that could benefit the rural
enterprise sector tremendously. At the same time, those instruments and
products developed should be updated and revised continuously to keep their
usefulness.
(g) The Bank Group's project supervision missions should be staffed adequately
with technical staff/consultants throughout-implementation. Sufficient budget
should be allocated for this purpose. This was unfortunately not the case.
(h) Financial institutions should strengthen their expertise in subproject
evaluation including adequate selection criteria, market assessment, cost
estimates, realistic implementation schedules, subborrowers' implementation
and repayment capabilities., counterpart funding requirement and availability,
and awareness of environnmental issues. However, even if these issues were
fully addressed by financial institutions, enterprises undertaking projects in
the context of limited finanicial and enterprise reforms would still face many
constraints under the current national policy framework. The Bank Group
should recognize these constraints in project preparation and try to seek
solutions. If the Bank Group realizes that these issues could not be solved
with one individual investment project in the absence of macroeconomic
structural adjustments, then they should be clearly spelled out in project
documents in order to avoid unrealistic expectations.
(i)  For lines of credit operations using financial intermediaries to be successful,
some basic structural changes in the financial and enterprise sectors are
needed:
(i)  stricter enforcement of laws, such as commercial banking law, company
law, and bankruptcy law;
(ii) enterprise behavior in terms of honoring loan repayment obligations
and ensuring timely repayment;
(iii) institutional behavior and performance standards of the financial
institutions, such as subproject selection criteria, appraisal standards,
portfolio managemenit, adequacy of bad debt provision, etc.; and



- 22 -
(iv) local governments' attitudes to financial institutions and enterprises, i.e.
local governments treat banks as conduits of funds to enterprises, and at
the same time, they are more concerned with enterprises' social
obligations and taxes rather than causing enterprises to repay bank
loans.

TABLE 9: ECONOMIC COSTS AND BENEFITS
The PFIs reevaluated financial and economic viability of nine subprojects after
subproject completion. Both EIRR1 and FIRR were required to be above 12 percent,
according to the project document. Based on a sample of nine subprojects, the ex-post EIRR
and FIRR were all lower than originally estimated due mainly to increasing input prices and
decreasing output prices.
Subprojects were selected for calculation of ex-post EIRR and FIRR based on the
following criteria: (a) a minimum of two subprojects from each of the PFIs; (b)
enterprises from different sectors and different cities of the three project areas; (c) an even
mix of successful and less successful subprojects; (d) half in repayment stage; and (e)
subprojects of different project sizes. The PFIs reevaluated financial and economic viability
of nine subprojects after subproject comapletion. The nine subprojects accounted for about
10 percent by number of commercial subprojects and 13 percent of the loan/credit amount
disbursed to the commercial subprojects.

-34-                     APPENDIX A
APPENDIX A: ICR MISSION'S AIDE MEMOIRE
1.    A World Bank mission, comprised of Helen Chan, Naiqin Lu and Amanda Hagy,
met with SSTC and all participating agencies for about 17 days between June 20 and
July 25, 1997 to review project status and initiate ICR preparation. The mission visited all
three project areas, accompanied by Mmes. Ouyang Xiaoguang and Tong Heifeng of the
Spark Program Office of SSTC and Mr. Yang Jun of ISTIC. A wrap-up meeting was held
with SSTC (attended by Messrs. Zhang Jingan, Director of Center of Rural Enterprises
and Wang Xiaofang, Director of Department of Rural Enterprises and the Spark Project
Office) and project entities from all project areas on July 25, attended also by Mr. Mo
Xiaolung, World Bank Department of MOF. Copies of the draft aide memoire were
distributed for discussion. Follow-up meetings were held on July 28 and August 7 to
confirm ICR requirements. The mission would like to extend its thanks to all project
agencies for the cooperation and warm hospitality. The following summarizes main
findings and recommendations of the mission, to be confirmed by a post-mission letter
from Bank management.
2.    The mission visited four subprojects, the Changchun training center, and the
Yanbien Science and Technology Information Research Institute in Jilin province; four
subprojects in Chongming; seven subprojects, Jiangyin training center and a pilot case
study in Jiangsu province. Factory visits insights are in Annex 3, 4 and 5. This is a
comprehensive supervision mission and it provides the mission and the accompanying
SSTC staff a deeper understanding and insights into project performance after six years
of implementation.
Project Status
3;    Except for a few small subconnponents, the project is basically completed. The
project will be closed at the end of September 1997. A small amount in the loan account
left unutilized and would not be needed any more, thus SSTC would request its
cancellation before the closing date. With the complexity of the technical assistance
components, the results thus far have demonstrated that they have been quite
successfully. Performances of subproejects which account for over 90 percent of the
project amount have been mixed. Nonetheless, all participating entities have put in a
sufficient amount of hard work to bring the project to completion and continuous efforts
would be needed in the future to fully realize its benefits.
Preparation of an Implementation Completion Report (ICR) and Processing
Schedule
4.    At the end of each Bank project, the Bank, together with its counterpart agencies,
will prepare an ICR to summarize project results, identify implementation problems and



-35 -                      APPENDIX A
to gather lessons learned in the preparation and implementation of the project. The ICR
would consist of the following sections:
* evaluation summary, written by Bank staff;
* project implementation assessment, written by Bank staff;
* statistical tables, prepared by Bank staff with inputs from participating agencies;
* Annex A, Borrower's own write-up and evaluation of the project;
* Annex B, ICR mission aide memoire;
* Annex C, List of subprojects financed under the project; and
* Annex D, ABC, CIB and JPTIC's comparative (actual and projected) financial
statements for years ending December 31, 1991 to June 1997.
5.    The processing schedule is:
* September 30, 1997; SSTC submits Annex A to the Bank and all data relevant for
ICR preparation. The mission has discussed a set of 13 tables to be prepared by all
participating agencies;
* November/December 1997; ICR mission;
* January 1998, Bank sends complete ICR to MOF and SSTC for clearance; and
* March 1998, ICR goes to the Board of the Bank.
6.    From discussions and documentation prepared for the mission and for publicity of
the Spark project (such as the World Bank Spark project conference held in February
1997), we noted that coverage is not comprehensive to reflect fully results of some
subcomponents. Some of the subcomponents are very small, like the precommercial
subprojects, pilot case studies, instructional packages and some consultant services
development; yet they offer substantive insights to small rural industrial enterprises
development. Thus in the ICR preparation, all identifiable subcomponents should be
included and evaluated.
Accomplishments, Issues and Suggestions
Project Management and Implementation
7.    Project Implementation by National Agencies. Since the beginning of the
project, World Bank Spark program offices were set up at all agencies (national,
provincial, municipal, cities, county, local STCs, financial intermediaries, ISTIC and
local STICs,l etc.) and there are special units at SSTC to work on different technical
assistance (TA) components. In the past six years, the Spark Program Office of SSTC has
organized seven project implementation workshops with all participating agencies from
State Science and Technology Commission (SSTC), Science and Technology Commissions (STC) at lower levels,
Institute of Science and Technology Information Center (ISTIC) at the national level and Science and Technology
Infornation Centers ISTIC) at lower levels.



*- 36 -                     APPENDIX A
Beijing and from different cities and municipalities of the three project areas to
coordinate work among different provinces and agencies, discuss project progress,
identify issues and seek solutions. The last one was held in October 1996 concentrating
on repayment issues. With these workshops and close coordination among all agencies,
the project has been progressing satisfactory, particularly the technical assistance
components as they involve not only agencies at the national and provincial levels, but
many municipal and county level agencies.
8.    Manpower Requirements After Completion of the Bank Project. The Bank's
Spark project is closed to completion; yet there remain a lot of work to be continued by
SSTC and provincial STCs in the three project areas, such as:
*  applications and promotion of knowledge acquired and systems setup thus far on
management information system (MIS), technical information (TI), technology
evaluation (TE), consultant services, training and instructional packages;
*  loan repayment issues; and
*  utilization of second generation funds.
The Spark project still has a long way to go for all national agencies. SSTC and local
STCs will have to work closely with financial institutions to monitor subloan repayments.
Utilization of second generation funds should follow same project objectives and
selection criteria.
9.    Suggestions. Thus, it is important that the Spark project offices at the national
and provincial STCs to be retained for a few years to carry out these work. The Spark
project offices should be staffed adequately to meet the challenge of the work ahead.
Technical Assistance, Spark Training Component
10.   Among the various activities and subcomponents under this component, the
mission noted two subcomponents for further discussions.
11.   Training Centers. The Chongming training center is very well organized and
utilized. Training offered are relevant to rural enterprises. The objective of building the
auxiliary building for the Changchuin training center is for training and not for
dormitories. Thus we requested that its objective to be strictly retained. As for Jiangyin
training center, there are a few issues:
*  training offered in the training center are mainly language, basic computer
applications, accounting and hotel services. There are few training for TVEs in
project management, project risk analysis, marketing, etc. As our purpose of
supporting a training center in Jianigyin is for TVEs, we asked Jiangyin STC to pay
more attention to TVE training;
*  full utilization of the building should be the objective. The objectives of the new
auxiliary building are for training and to house different laboratories financed under
the project, such as the microcomputer/electronics laboratory, language laboratory,



-37 -                     APPENDIX A
instructional equipment and teaching material manufacturing & bookbinding
equipment. The new building had completed construction and started operations since
June 1996; yet over half are still vacant. Out of the five floors, three floors are
designated for instructors' offices. Some office spaces are rented for commercial
businesses. Only 1/5 of the floor space are now used for training purposes. Thus we
requested the original objectives be kept and the new building of the Jiangyin training
center be better utilized.
* Currently, all training are conducted at the original older building, constructed about
10 years ago and it is equipped with equipment financed by the Bank project. This
building is desperately in need of some basic maintenance, such as painting.
* It is amazed to learn that while the new building is still half vacant, another building
is under construction for the training center. The building under construction will be
used mainly for recreational purposes, canteen, etc. While it is not financed with Bank
fund, it raises the issue of better utilization of scarce capital resources.
12.   Instructional Packages. A  few  instructional packages, using multimedia
technology, were developed, such as the CNC machine tools and the HRD management
in Jiangsu, modified starch in Jilin, the DPC application in cotton production in Beijing
and the household electronic appliance in Chongming-all are very relevant for TVE
development, yet need to be applied and promoted. And these activities should be carried
out as soon as the instructional packages are completed and they should be relevant to
many provinces in China. If there are problems with any one instructional package, such
as the household electronic appliance, explanations should be provided in the ICR.
13.   Requests. For all instructional packages, the mission requested following
additional information:
*  total cost of production, including Bank fund;
 starting and completion dates;
* objectives of the instructional package (i.e. why this is necessary);
* final beneficiaries;
* basic contents of the packages (in couple of sentences);
* current applications, if any;
* what need to be done before the packages could be fully utilized by final
beneficiaries; and
* current plan of future application and promotion (with dates).
Technology Information (TI) Component
14.   Business and Technology Information is becoming increasingly important for
enterprises as markets are becoming very competitive. This is particularly important for
rural industrial enterprises to have easy access to reliable business and technical
information at a low cost. The business and technical information network that the project
helped established and the science and technology information institutes that the project
helped equipped would be very useful if they are properly maintained and fully utilized.



-38 -                    APPENDIX A
The mission visited Yanbien Science and Technology Information Research Institute in
Jilin and found its work very worth supporting. The Chongming Technology Information
Center had also provided many services to its clients. These institutes would need
continuous proper maintenance (computer hardware and software, network and staff
training) and management for them to provide useful business and technology
information services to rural industrial enterprises. There should also be more
communications and cooperation between STICs in different provinces and within
provinces to avoid duplication of work and waste of both capital and human resources on
technology and research work.
15.   Fee-Based Information Consultant Services. One objective of the TI
component is to develop fee-based local consulting services, directly responsive to TVEs
requests, for rural industrial enterprises throughout the SSTC/ISTIC system. The Spark
program office has paid special attention to its development. Local consultant services to
solve specific problems of rural enterprises were tested with a few case studies, one is
Chongming (technology issue) and one in Jiangsu (marketing and sales). Objective
evaluation and documentation of lessons learned would be very useful for future TVE
developments.
16.  Industrial Technical Evalualion (TE). Two manuals on technical evaluation,
one for trainers and one for trainees were completed by the end of 1996. The purpose of
these manuals is to provide SSTC and the Spark training centers tools to train and
upgrade STCs' staff nationwide. Their completion is a step closer to achieve that goal.
17.   Pilot Case Studies. Two case studies, one in Henan and one in Chongming, using
knowledge acquired through TE were applied to Spark intensive area and Spark small
district area. Both case studies were coimpleted in the past 12 months.
18.   Suggestions. The mission strongly suggested that SSTC to organize training as
soon as possible with the two TE manmaals to train lower-level STCs staff. The pilot case
studies would provide lessons learned in their applications in both Spark intensive area
and Spark small district area. Lessons learned from these pilot studies should also be
documented and would be very useful ifor future TVE development.
19.   Books Prepared. A  few  books relevant to rural industrial enterprises
development have recently been prepared under the Bank project that deserve more
attention. They are:
* Human Resources Development Management in Medium and Rural Enterprises
(developed with the HRD instructional package);
*  Guidelines for Consultant Services for TVEs in China;
* Technical Evaluation of Industrial Projects for TVEs, trainers' and trainees' manuals;
and
*  Technical appraisal of TVE in Spark Small Districts (developed with the Chongming
case studies).



- 39 -                   APPENDIX A
20.   Suggestions. After their completion, it is time for dissemination. Hopefully they
would be utilized throughout China, by rural industrial enterprises, by teaching institutes
(like Spark training centers and other colleges), and by STCs in their assistance to rural
enterprises. The mission suggested SSTC and local STCs put sufficient emphasis on their
immediate dissemination. Then sufficient resources would be needed in the near future
for revisions and upgrading.
21.   Management Information System (MIS). The MIS was developed a few years
ago for management of the national Spark program. Like the TI, it should be used,
revised and upgraded continuously; otherwise the system would be obsolete fairly
quickly. It should be used not only for Spark project applications, but also to track results,
i.e. financial and project performances after funds were utilized. Without these latter data,
the MIS would not be as useful for Spark program planning and for policy guidance for
SSTC as it supposes to be.
22.   In short, there are a lot of accomplishment with the TA components. Yet their real
success would depend very much on future efforts of SSTC to disseminate, promote and
apply whatever had been developed. IF these products were not utilized timely, their
usefulness would diminish very quickly.
Line of Credit
23.   Issues of Financial Institutions. This project is basically a line of credit
operations through financial institutions with special emphasis on the national Spark
program and rural industrial enterprises. Funds under the line of credit were fully
committed and over 90 percent disbursed. During factory visits and discussions with
ABC branches in particular, the impression that the mission got was that subproject
supervision, including monitoring of loan principal repayments, loan rescheduling, and
assisting enterprises in solving nonrepayment issues, seem to be secondary to project
preparation and evaluation. For example, loans are not rescheduled in spite of
nonrepayment of over a year. ABC staff that we met were not familiar with project status.
It translated into the fact that proper project management procedures are not in place that
allow smooth transition when responsibilities are transferred. Thus we felt that the four
financial intermediaries under the project have not performed satisfactory as financial
intermediaries (FIs):
(a) financial restructuring of banks in the past few years, e.g. the commercialization of
banks, ABC becoming three financial institutions instead of one, affected continuity;
(b) the chain of command and control within the Chinese system, i.e., ABC and CIB
branches report directly to their respective headquarters in Beijing, while the Spark
project is under the direct administration of the provincial government, even though
the line of credit is relent through provincial financial intermediaries. The
provincial/municipal governments bear the responsibility of loan repayment to the
Central Government, loan repayment to the Bank is not directly the responsibility
ABC and CIB branches. Thus, in case of subloan nonrepayment and defaults, ABC



-40 -                    APPENDIX A
and CIB branches could delay repayments to provincial governments which bear the
responsibility of loan repaymenats to the Central Government. With subloan
repayments starting in 1995 and 1996 and loan repayment on the Spark project
started in 1996, financial burdens on provincial government started to appear; and
(c) loan repayment concepts by enterprises still weak and inadequate legal enforcement
of loan nonrepayment indirectly encouraged enterprises' actions. Thus, triangular
debts are common. Even enterprises operating profitably and have ability to repay do
not repay. Funds are diverted for new project investment or for working capital
purposes.
24.   During subproject preparation and approvals, the FIs were actively involved; yet
as soon as funds are fully committed and disbursed, the FIs have taken a back seat. This
is particularly obvious with both ABCs in Jiangsu and Shanghai. Both ABC branches had
prepared nothing for the mission, did not accompany the mission during factory visits and
did not attempt to answer any questions raised by the mission, least to say, being totally
uncooperative. CIB has its own special problem of being restructured in 1995 and its
subbranches being closed. Thus it was not able to supervise its subprojects effectively.
Among the four FIs, the Jilin Trust and Investment Corporation appears to be most
concerned of its subprojects.
25.   The fact that ABC branches did not accompany mission factory visits had left
many questions unresolved. The mission received conflicting and controversial answers
to questions raised during Jiangsu factory visits, such as loan repayment terms (7 years
terms with 3 years grace instead of 3 to 5 years terms) and non principle repayment as
agreements between factories and ABC (factories said agreements reached vs. no
agreements stated by ABC). Should Jiangsu ABC staff were at the factory visits, these
would be clarified and the mission would have better understanding of the situation. This
understanding is also beneficial to SSTC, provincial and local STCs staff who
accompanied the mission on factory visits.
26.   Suggestions. The mission requested that SSTC and provincial authorities bring
these issues to the attention of ABC head office in Beijing and provincial ABC
presidents. This is a serious problem in the Spark project as ABC components in both
project areas amount to US$60 million, more than half of the project. Subproject
successes and failures in Jiangsu and Shanghai could affect evaluation of the entire
project performance.
27.   Willingness to Honor Legal Obligations by Borrowing Enterprises. It is so
obvious that new project development and civil construction take priority over principal
loans repayments. A few enterprises visited have modern offices with construction on
going, yet not repaying their current loans.
28.   Suggestions. Close supervision by financial institutions. Fl should include some
financial ratio requirements in future loan agreements with borrowing enterprises, and if
well managed, it may help. In the meantime, SSTC should, as early as possible, organize



-41-                        APPENDIX A
some training for borrowing enterprises of their legal obligations in the financial terms.
This type of training should be offered continuously and be reiterated in all management
courses for rural industrial enterprises.

ANNEX 2: FACTORY VISITS IN JILIN PROVINCE
(JUNE 23-25, 1997)
B-21-JEDI Siping Hongzhui Maclhinery General Plant
Subloan of US$2.353 million, approved on April 25,1995
Status. Subproject construction was on schedule, production started at the end of 1995,
and full capacity reached in May 1997. Under the subproject, the advanced small
continuous steel casting and rolling technology and equipment (manufactured by
Shanghai Heavy Machinery Plant (SHMP) based on the imported know-how) were
procured. This technology and equiprnent, recommended by Ministry of Metallurgical
Industry for dissemination (an important element objective of the Spark program), have
the advantages of energy saving and high processing efficiency, with which the plant's
production cost is down, product quality and competitiveness improved. During
equipment installation and trial operation, SHMP and Bao Tou Steel Corporation
provided technical support and training. The plant has already manufactured four similar
equipment for its clients for promotion of the proven advanced technology and
equipment. With the subproject as the loundation, the plant has developed into one of the
50 largest enterprises in Jilin province, with sales of Y 80 million and net profit of Y 10
million in 1996. Its productivity is higher than most steel SOEs of the province and is
ranked as second. It is the largest export earner in Siping city. The plant has grown into
Hong Zhui Steel Group Company, having 10 subordinated factories and 1,650
employees, compared with 2 factories and 500 employees in 1994; thus contributing
substantially to local economic development. It has repaid Y 4.2 million of the subloan
and the subloan will be fully repaid by end of 1998.
Observations. The mission noted that there were heavy smoke generated from the
furnaces during production and asked about environmental protection measures. The
plant said that it has been paying penalties in not meeting basic environmental measures.
NEPA and provincial environmental agency knew about. However, domestic equipment,
such as dust collectors do not reached international standard and NEPA is fully aware of
the deficiency of those equipment. Consequently, dust collector and other equipment are
being ordered from Japan to treat waste dust emission and the equipment will be
delivered at the end of 1997.
Recommendations. The mission reiteraeted the importance of environmental controls and
asked the plant and the accompanying government officials to make sure that after
installation of the imported dust collector, it would be operated as specified by the
manufacturer. If the dust collector is not turned on (to save electricity), environmental
issues remain.
B-17-JEDI Dunhua Hua Kang Pharmaceutical Factory
Subloan of US$954,000, approved on July 20, 1994
Status. Subproject construction was on schedule and completed at the end of 1996.
Commercial operations also started in 1996. During implementation, the factory received



-44-                     APPENDIX A
useful support from relevant R & D institutes and universities to help establish GMP and
to ensure quality of its herb medicine which uses indigenous herb plants for specific heart
related decease. The factory received substantive technical support (an important element
of the Spark program) from different agencies:
* the new production workshops were designed and supervised by Wuhan
Pharmaceutical Design Institute, certified by State Pharmaceutical Administration to
ensure the required cleaning production conditions;
* the process technology was assisted by Provincial Pharmaceutical Research Institute;
and
* the prescription of the herb medicine was provided by Changchun Pharmaceutical
University.
Its market share has been increasing in the past year. The factory estimated that 65
percent of the installed production capacity could be reached in 1997, 90 percent in 1998,
and 100 percent in 1999. Its production value increased to Y 150 million in 1996 from
Y 100 million in 1994 and is expected to reach Y 200 million in 1997 and Y 300 million
at full capacity. The factory has diversified its products and used acquired technologies
(through this project) and managerial skills to form a group company with six factories,
covering medicine, biotechnology, pesticide, animal medicine etc. In addition,
implementation of this subproject has helped spur development of herb planting and
packaging materials and technology in the province. The factory has started to repay its
loan-Y 2 million in 1996, Y 3 million this year, and the rest will be repaid according to
schedule.
Observations and Recommendations. The factory is clean and appears well managed.
The mission cautioned that continual product development is important for the factory to
maintain and increase its market share; and quality improvement is crucial for its
competitiveness.
A-08-JEDI Korean Nationality Aluminum Product Factory
Subloan of US$2.5 million, approved on August 8, 1993
Status and Issues. Subproject implementation suffered serious delay due to poor
planning and availability of counterpart funds at the right time. Production could not
proceed because critical production equipment were not in place. While the new
workshop construction is almost fmiished, the mechanical unit of the aluminum rolling
machine (to produce aluminum film) has been installed, the imported electronic control
unit and the domestic cutting machines have been delivered; yet the hydraulic unit
(cooling system and fire extinguishing system of the rolling machine) have not been
delivered, and the electrical cables and the control panel of the rolling machines have not
even been ordered.
The delay has resulted in expensive overrun. According to factory estimates, another
Y 14.58 million would be needed to complete the subproject.



-45 -                   APPENDIX A
Reasons for Implementation Delay
Feasibility study was completed in 1992. Bank approved the subproject in August
1993 after substantial discussions. Contracts with the major supplier equipment in
China were signed in 1993. The contract requested a 30 percent down payment after
signing. Due to insufficient funds at that time, the factory paid only 10 percent as
down payment rather than the agreed 30 percent, thus rendering the contract
ineffective. The rapid price increases between 1992 to 1995, the equipment supplier
had increased the equipment prices substantially. In addition, this is a small contract
in comparison to contracts of large companies, plus the noncompliance of down
payment, so the equipment supplier had not given it much attention; and the
manufacturing process took much longer than the contract specified. However, the
factory had no legal ground to pursue as it did not honor its obligation first.
* The factory wanted to save import duties (imposed at the end of 1995); thus ordered
and imported equipment from England prior to when it could actually be installed and
utilized.
Realizing its problems, the factory has made the right move in recruiting a factory
manager from the Harbin Aluminum Product Factory, an advanced and very competitive
aluminum products producer, to manage the factory.
Observations. The factory, mainly due to its financial difficulties, has been merged into
Yangbian Aluminum Group Company in 1994. Its products, different widths of
aluminum foil sheets, were not competitive due to its low quality and were able to meet
market demand of wider widths. The subproject was supposed to introduce new
technology and equipment for the factory to improve its product quality and market share.
Yet due to prolonged delay in project implementation, project has substantial cost
overrun and in the meantime, market demand changed and much more competitors as
well.
Recommendations
*  The mission asked the factory to approach its sponsor, local banks and local
government for financing support. Sufficient fund would be needed to complete the
project without further delay. Considering the potential markets of the products and
conditions of new workshop and technology, the subproject could still be financially
viable, provided that funding for the financing gap and the subsequent working
capital needed would be available on a timely basis.
* JPTIC should reschedule loan repayment immediately based on current estimated
project cash flow.
*  The factory, together with JPTIC and Jilin STC, should analyze market more
realistically and review its strategies to see whether its products could meet market
demand, in view of the rapidly changing demand in aluminum products.



-46-                     APPENDIX A
Lessons Learned
*  The Bank should have been duly informed of the change in legal status of the
subproject entity. The Bank should also have been informed of its problems during
implementation so that the Bank could help addressing the problems.
*  Enterprise should honor all their legal obligations. Should the factory had paid the
supplier according to contractual requirements, cost overrun would be lower and its
equipment might have been manufactured according to schedule as stated in the
contract.
B-19-JEDI  YanJi Plastic Product Factory (Plastic Bottle for Mineral Water)
Subloan of US$721,000 million, approved on April 25, 1995
Status. Imported equipment was installed in April 1996 and trial production started in
May 1996. The imported equipment is relatively advanced-integrating plastic injection
with stretching and blowing. Equipment is working well and the bottles are attractive.
However, production and sale were limited in 1996 due to changing market demand. In
order to remain in business, the factory had no choice but to invest in a new mold in
1997. This has put additional financial burden on the factory, seriously affecting its
financial ability to repay. The subloan agreement was signed in June 1995 and principal
repayment should start in July 1996. However, as the factory was struggling to stay in
business in 1996 and the new mold has just started commercial operations in mid-1997,
the factory is suffering financial loss in the past two years.
Issues
*  Rapidly Changing Market. Based on project feasibility study which was completed
in early 1994, 1.5 liter (45 g) bottle was in demand, thus one set of mold for 1.5 liter
bottle was imported in 1996 at the cost of US$60,000. However in 1996, market
demand for smaller (23 g) bottles of mineral water is much more than larger bottles
(45 g). Starting in January 1997, the factory spent another Y 120,000 to work with
Dalian Polytechnical University to develop a mold for smaller bottles. After five
months of testing and adjustment, this new mold was put into operation in May 1997.
In June 1997, production volume has reached about 60 percent of the designed
capacity, and could further increase to 80 percent by the summer of 1997.
*  Stiff Competition. With the increase of disposal income in China, domestic demand
of bottling water increased substantially in the 1994 to 1997 period. Many bottling
companies had sprung up during this period; thus market potential for this factory is
not as promising as forecast in the subproject appraisal report. In 1997, there are 26
bottling factories in Yanji city for mineral water.
*  Low Profit Margins. Net profit is only Y 0.06 per bottle for small bottles. Higher for
the larger bottle, Y 0.54 per bottle, thus sale volume has to be very large to be
profitable. The factory has started to export its bottles to North Korea for bottling
mineral water and import the bottled water for sale in the domestic market. In so
doing, it will earn Y 1.9 per small bottle of mineral water rather than Y 0.6 per bottle.



-47 -                    APPENDIX A
Observations
*  Unless actions are taken as soon as possible to increase its share for both bottle sizes
and possibly venture into other businesses, it would be difficult for the factory to be
financially viable in the competitive market of bottling water.
*  Even though the factory was nolt able to repay since July 1996, JPTIC has not
rescheduled the loan nor work out any plan with the factory to solve the problem.
Thus JPTIC is to work with the factory to reschedule the loan immediately based on
its revised market strategy and project cash flow in the next few years.
Lessons Learned
*  Market forecast should consider possible new entrants into the market, rather than
assuming that market will remain status quo.
*  Production technology is relatively simple and is very easy for new entrants. Thus
investment in single product with simple technology should be studied very carefully
before investment. Diversifying its product base may be the only choice for long term
survival for this factory.



-48 -                    APPENDIX A
ANNEX 3: FACTORY VISITS IN CHONGMING ISLAND,
SHANGHAI (JULY 14-16, 1997)
A-07-SHA   Shanghai No. 1 Subsidiary Glove Plant
Subloan of US$212,000, approved on January 10, 1993
Status. Before this project, the plant produced rubber gloves soaked in PVC agent only
once. In this project, the process technology of soaking rubber gloves twice was jointly
developed with Shanghai No. 2 Glove Factory. This will improve glove quality and
competitiveness. Trial production started in 1993 and the demand has been strong,
especially along the coastal provinces. However, its products are mainly exported to
Japan. To meet demand, the plant is now producing around the clock in three shifts. The
plant prefers exports to domestic sales lto avoid problems of triangular debts. In 1996, the
plant formed a joint venture with a Japanese firm to fuither expand glove production. By
the end of 1966, the plant had fully repaid the subloan.
Observations and Suggestions
* The mission reminded the plant that pollution control should be closely monitored as
it uses a lot of chemical materials in its production, though there is no obvious sign of
pollution, like leakage and nontreated waste water running into nearly streams, in the
plant.
* This production process was developed with Shanghai #2 Glove Factory. The plant
understands that there is another factory in China producing similar rubber gloves. As
technology is very simple and could be easily developed, the mission suggested that
the plant to diversify its market base rather than relying solely on exports to Japan and
at the samne time, diversify its product base for its long term development.
B-03-SHA   Shanghai Baoli New-Style Packaging Material Factory (A-PET)
Subloan of US$2.367 million, approved on October 26, 1993
Status. The subproject is aimed to produce environmental friendly "A-PET" packaging
material to replace PVC which the E,uropean Union (EU) announced in 1989 that
European countries would not allow imports of food packaged in PVC containers in a few
years. During the testing and production of the new packaging materials, Shanghai Plastic
Packaging Corporation provided assistance in all aspects of production, technology and
marketing. The designed capacity of the subproject is 3,000 tons/year. Trial production
started in 1995. However, due to lack of government policies and regulations to ban use
of PVC, demand for A-PET packaging rnaterials is far from satisfactory; consequently, its
production is much lower appraisal estimates. The factory has been operating at a loss
since 1995. Currently, its products are maainly used as indirect export, such as packaging
for exported food and clothes etc. With. the price gap between PET and PVC narrowed
from a previous 2.5 times to about 50 percent higher now, the factory is expecting a better
financial future in the next few years. It plans to produce 1,000 tons of "A-PET"



- 49-                   APPENDIX A
packaging material in 1997, while the break even point is 50 percent installed production
capacity of 3,000 tons per year.
Observations and Suggestions
*  When the EU regulations came out in early 1989, seven (including this factory)
factories in seven different provinces rushed to set up similar A-PET packaging
material production lines simultaneously in China. Thus the factory would face stiff
competition even if the market as strong as predicted. All seven plants had completed
installation of equipment and trial production, however, market for A-PET packaging
materials has not been established in China yet, and the raw materials, PET, is much
more expensive than PVC. Thus some plants had stop production already. Except for
the Baoli plant, the other six plants are not independent legal factories and they
survive on activities in other workshops of the same enterprise.
*  Chinese regulations on food hygiene and sanitation and environmental protection,
thus far, have not catch up with international standards and markets yet. Thus it is
important that the Chinese Government to pass relevant food hygiene and sanitation
and environmental protection regulations to phase out PVC for food, pharmaceutical
and toy packaging first before A-PET packaging materials will take off in China.
* The mission suggested to local government officials to raise awareness of provincial
government and eventually to the national government of the EU regulations and the
world market. If China is going to join GATT and to export any food and
pharmaceutical products and toys, they will have to be packaged in non-PVC and
non-ODS packing materials.
*  Even though the factory is still projecting good market in the next two year, the
mission found it difficult to have optimistic financial projection for this factory. The
factory has been delinquent in its loan repayment, but ABC Shanghai had not pay
much attention to the problem yet. Shanghai ABC should work with the factory to
reschedule subloan repayment and Shanghai STC should provide any technical help
to the factory for its survival.
B-06-SHA   Shanghai Far East Refrigerator Factory
Subloan of US$650,000, approved on May 8, 1995
Status and Issues. The subproject is to finance non-CFC technology and equipment (two
vacuum forming machines, HFC 134a refrigerant filling equipment, foam machines etc.)
for the production of 100,000 sets of CFC-free refrigerators per year. Subproject
implementation was delayed due mainly to the change of production process caused by
the equipment supplier. The testing and adjustment of equipment will be finished by
September 1997 and trial production is expected to start in October. The factory will
break even if it reached 70 percent of the installed capacity.
In 1996, due to financial difficulties, the factory was acquired by Chongming County
Industry & Commerce Corporation, with the factory retained its name. Correspondingly,
Shanghai Shangling Refrigerator Corporation provided technical assistance and

eventually formed a joint venture with the factory in April 1997 on a 50:50 basis. With

Currently, the factory could not repay its loan, but should be able to start repayment two
years after commercial production.
Observations
*  Despite assistance from its joint-venture partner and its mother company, the factory
is currently not able to repay its loan. But with a new manager from its joint-venture
partner and its CFC-free refrigerators will bear the Shangling brand (a famous

take two years after commercial opetrations. Thus Shanghai ABC should start working

* This subproject has supported the CFC phaseout efforts in China which is in response
to a promotion by the National Council of Light Industries.
A-08-SHA   Chongming Subsidiary Plant (for Production of Bearings)
Subloan of US$1.39 mil]Lion, approved on January, 12, 1993
Status and Issues. Trial production had started in 1994, but until today, only 20 percent
of the designed capacity was reached. The plant is in deep financial difficulties since
1994. Its debts and daily operations are supported by the township Industry and Trade
Company (Jialong). The low capacity utilization was due basically to a mismatch of
capacity in different pieces of equipment in the production process. The plant produces
mainly low-valued small and medium bearings. It now explores the market of higher-
valued larger bearings and precision bearings.
Since 1996, technical experts from Halrbin were invited by Jialong to work as technical
supervisors in the plant. Other technical support, the plant hopes that the Harbin experts
would open the northeast market for them. The plant's financial situation has improved
slightly. Production value is expected to increase to Y 14 million in 1997 from Y 12
million in 1996, but the break-even production value is Y 18 million. The technical
experts from Harbin believe that the plant would not be able to repay the Bank loan in
another three years. The township government expects the plant to break-even next year,
but could only afford interest payments.
Observations
* The plant is in serious financial difficulties and in spite of technical assistance from
experts and its mother company, the mission had doubts on its long termn financial
viability. The top and immediate priority is not to put additional investment into the
plant as it had requested, but Shanghai STC, Jialong, Shanghai ABC and the plant
should review the plant's current production, its market perspectives, and its
competitiveness immediately. This i.s to be followed by a detailed financial analysis to



- 51 -                   APPENDIX A
see whether the plant could actually break-even in the next two without bringing
continuous financial burden to local government, to see whether it could bring any
value added to the local economy and to see whether it could ever repay its loan from
its production. Should this detail analysis showed that value added of the plant is
negative, an alternative (closing, changed production, if possible, joint venture if a
partner could be located) could be evaluated.
* In the meantime, the plant needs to rectify its unmatched manufacturing capacity and
to modify its production mix with higher priced products.
* There is a successful TVE producing bearings with similar technology under the
Spark project in the Jiangsu province. Thus the plant could consult this Jiangsu TVE
to learn their experience and explore other alternatives.

ANNEX 4: FACTORIES VISITS IN JIANGSU PROVINCE (JULY
16-22, 1997)
A-02-CIBA Jianghai Capacitor Factory
Subloan of US$2.5 million, approved on December 4, 1991
Status and Its Sponsor. The subloan is to finance imported equipment for the
manufacturing of miniature aluminum electrolytic capacitor of medium and high voltage.
Commercial production started in 1995 and full production capacity has been reached.
The capacitor workshop has become one of the main workshops of the factory. With
continuous close cooperation with Xi'an JiaoTong University, Fudan University, and
universities in Jiangsu since 1991, a series of new products was developed. The factory
has now become the largest revenue earner in the past four years in the capacitor sector in
China, with Y 160 million earnings in 1996 and an estimated Y 220 million in 1997. The
factory has been profitable in the past 16 years. In the coming three years, the factory
expects a 20 percent annual growth in the capacitor production and revenues. Both its
domestic and international customers are mainly big companies with famous brand
products.
The factory has set up a joint production plant in Xiamen, to disseminate its technology,
marketing and management skills (knowledge dissemination-an important objective in
the national Spark program). In addition, the plant plans to set up a high voltage capacitor
plant in Mienyang city, Sichuan province, to help develop the local advanced capacitor
production (technology diffusion). As a strong TVE in Jiangsu, the plant has more access
to loans from different banks and its debt equity ratio is currently about 55 percent. The
plant has started its subloan repayment according to the set schedule. Workers' average
annual income is about Y 10,000, compared with Y 5,000 of local residents.
Comments. The factory is a strong collective, with many years' experience in capacitor
production, development and marketing. Its strong background helps ensure the
successful implementation of the subproject, while the subproject has also spurred fast
development of the factory in recent years.
Lessons Learned. Strong enterprise, in terms of management, vision and finances;
technical support from R&D in new production development; right environment, in terms
of locality, local support and manpower (sufficiently qualified, otherwise, relying solely
on the job training would make projectl vulnerable), sufficient counterpart funding at the
right time and realistic market analysis are basic elements to a successful project.
B-09-CIBA Rugao Glass Fiber Factory
Subloan of US$772,000, approved on February 8, 1993
Status and Issues. The subloan agreement was signed in May, 1993. The subloan is for
the financing of manufacturing equipment in reinforcing netlike fiberglass cloth for
grinding wheel. This product would fill the gap in domestic market. The products has



-53 -                    APPENDIX A
reached advanced international standards and has become one of the leading products of
the factory. Its sales reached Y 22 million in 1996, and its production volume amounted
to about 40 percent of total production volume of the factory. The netlike fiberglass cloth
is very well accepted in both domestic and overseas markets, the factory has become the
largest fiberglass cloth producer in the country.
The subproject has laid solid foundation for the development of other related products in
the factory and has helped product diversification of the factory. Since 1994, the factory
has set up six joint ventures and form a "Jiu Ding Group company." However, its
manager lacks the understanding of financial and legal obligations. Even though the
subproject has been successfully implemented and the factory is profitable, the factory is
not repaying the subloan; rather, the factory spent its available funds in expansion
projects and physical construction. The subloan should have been fully repaid by May
1997 based on the subloan agreement, but as of July 1997, US$250,000 remained
outstanding. The factory manager agreed that based on project activities, the factory
could afford to repay the outstanding balance, yet he cited a few reasons why the factory
would not repay the loan yet:
* The factory was financially exhausted because it was forced to absorb three bankrupt
enterprises with 800 workers. There were practically no production and no revenues
in these three enterprises, but the factory has to pay their salaries and pay all social
benefits. The factory also bear the responsibility of restructuring these enterprises and
steer them into production activities.
3  Before 1995, fund for loan repayment is from net profit before income tax. Now loan
repayment is to be paid from net profit after income tax. The income tax rate is 33
percent. This project was approved in 1993. This change of policy added financial
burden to the factory which was not in financial forecast when the project was
prepared.
* Substantial devaluation of the yuan from Y 5.8 to US$1 in 1994 to Y 8.7 to US$1 in
1995 also added additional financial burden to the borrowing enterprise.
Recommendations
*  CIB Jiangsu should work immediately with the factory to work out a repayment plan.
The factory is basically profitable, even it is burdened with social responsibilities and
expansion projects. So repayment is really not an issue here.
* SSTC and provincial STC should organize some management training emphasizing
on financial and legal obligations for TVEs managers, not only in project areas, but
nationwide. It is particularly important in the TVE-clustered provinces, such as
Jiangsu. This subproject has played an important role in the fast development of the
factory and the local economy; yet due to other obligations and understandings, the
factory is putting financial burden on ABC and the finance bureaus. Thus it is
important that TVE managers to be aware they should honor all legal and financial
obligations.



-54 -                    APPENDIX A
* Chemical materials are used in the production process, thus environment protection
should be closely monitored by local environmental protection bureaus.
Lessons Learned. In this case, it is very clear that market analysis is too optimistic and
the factory (plus the other six A-PET plants as well) are marching ahead of time without
due consideration of market realities. It takes time for markets to respond to most
international regulations in China and it takes time for China to promulgate new
regulation, particularly it involves more than one national agencies. Plus it takes time for
any new regulations to be truly effective. Technologies change rapidly as well, so it is not
advisable to invest ahead of what is really marketable in the near future.
B-04-ABCA Taixing Granule Metallurgical Plant
Subloan of US$685,000, approved on February 8, 1993
Status and Issues. The subloan financed the orbital cold forging equipment (OCFE) and
technology. The production of cold precision forging parts started at the end of 1994.
With this subproject as the foundation, the plant later has acquired four larger OCFEs,
post-processing machines, testing equipment and CNC machines with other financial
resources, to establish comprehensive and functional production lines.
In May, 1997, it formed a joint venture with Pacific Precision Forging Ltd., Co. Its cold
precision forging gear and related components, mainly used for automobile, motorcycles,
etc. and its products are very competitive in the domestic market.
As ABC staff were not present at the factory visit, it was unclear to the mission what was
the real situation on its loan repaymeni: and ABC staff could not offer good explanations
later at the Jiangsu provincial branch either. The factory claimed that it had repaid part of
the subloan already, yet when they wanted prepayment at the end of 1994 (a lump sum
repayment), ABC Jiangsu told them to repay in full in 1998 with one lump sum. In view
of that, the plant used the available fund for other investment; thus delinquent on loan
repayment based on original schedule. But no one could confirm, including ABC,
whether this is the accurate account and whether a revised repayment schedule had been
worked out.
Observations
* It is a very successful subproject. The factory appears to be very managed.
Implementation was on schedule, its products are marketable and the factory has
grown into a large corporation. As it is profitable, repayment capability is not an
issue. Yet it is delinquent on its loan repayment which ABC Jiangsu could not
confirm nor offer an explanation. Nor ABC Jiangsu has initiated any appropriate
action on collection. The mission felt that current ABC staff really do not know what
is the real situation. It is a serious institutional issue on the part of ABC. Over the
course of next few months and during the ICR mission, the project team will pursue
on many issues (including other subprojects) that were uncovered during this
supervision mission.



- 55 -                   APPENDIX A
SSTC, together with Jiangsu STC, should promptly organize some management
training for TVE managers to raise their understanding and awareness in the
importance of legal and financial obligations. Just like some other successful and
profitable enterprises in the Spark project, if the enterprises do not repay and if the
financial institutions are not keeping up with repayment issues either, then the entire
Spark loan repayment will fall on provincial bureau. And SSTC would be not able to
utilize the resolving fund that is supposed to be generated from loan repayment.
Impact of the Spark project would subsequently be reduced.

B-03-CIBB Jinshi Group Corporation (a precommercial risk-sharing subproject)
Subloan of US$350,000, approved on March 5, 1996
Status and Progress. The subloan supported precommercial activities in the production
of L-Valine to be used by pharmaceutical factories. In 1993, Wuxi Jinhai Amino Acid
Ltd., a subsidiary of the Corporation, started production of L-isoleucine with support
from R&D institutes aimed to substitute imports. By 1997, its L-isoleucine production
has captured 80 percent of the domestic market. With the foundation of L-isoleucine,
Jinshi has further seek cooperation from universities and R&D institutes to develop
L-Valine production technology, also for import substitution. The precommercial
activities included: (i) to optimize fermentation through microcomputer control, (ii) to
develop new extraction refining technology to raise the extraction rate, and (iii) to test
acceptance of domestic users with pilot production. These activities had reduced the risks
for mass production of L-Valine with the new process technology. The Corporation
believed that the precommercial production are successful and is ready for commercial
operation. Equipment for commercial production are being adjusted and will put into
operation in Septernber.
Lessons Learned
*  The risk sharing venture is successful because the sponsor has prior experience in
similar type of production. Even it has its own technical expertise, it is also relying on
R&D institutes to provide basic research. For this new venture, it has recruited a
national reputed professor to be in charge of the fermentation experiment.
*  This is a large corporation and appears to be well managed. It has the capacity of
venturing into new technology without the assurance that it could be commercial
applicable.
* Management has the right perspective. It is ready to repay the loan when due and is
very appreciative of what Jiangsu has offered through the precommercial credit.
*  The mission was told that some precommercial activities are not successful because
project sponsor are not strong technically, management is weak and market has not
been studied carefully.

ANNEX 5: CASES OF SUBPROJECT DIFFUSION
A-02-CIBA Jianghai Capacitor Factory
Jianghai Capacitor Factory, a large electrolytic capacitor manufacturer in China,
had utilized the US$2,500,000 loan from the World Bank to produce a small-size high
voltage aluminum electrolytic capacitor using advanced technologies.
The equipment installation was completed in February 1995. After a successful
trial period, the new capacitor production reached its designed capacity by the end of
1995. The capacitors are now supplied to major television manufactures in China, such as
Changhong and Kangjia, and are also exported to the European and American markets.
At the same time, Jianghai is exploring the possibility of supplying capacitors to
manufacturers of energy-saving lights.
Using technologies learned in this project, the factory is building another large
aluminum electrolytic capacitor plant in Mianyang, Sichuan province. The new plant will
cover the entire southwestern market for Jianghai.
The profitability of this production line is much higher after the completion of the
project due to the proper selection of market and production processes. In addition,
Jianghai has gained experience in long termn planning, development of new products, and
negotiations of technology transfer contracts. More importantly, Jianghai has been
sharing its experience with other TVEs in all its dissemination activities.
B-11-CIBA  Jintan Cloisonn6 Enamel Processing Factory
Under the project, the factory imported an electroplating line to improve its
production capacity and product quality.
The project was completed in 1995. It has made Jintan the leading industrial
enterprise in local cloisonne production. Jintan Cloisonne has helped transforming a
group of small arts and crafts plants in Jintan from low-tech, inefficient, high-pollution
plants to more efficient and environmentally friendly ones. It has also taught these TVEs
how to design products and tailor produiction plans according to market demand.
The success of the Jintan Cloisonne project has had a very positive influence on
the local economy and employment. TIhe following are beneficiaries of Jintan Cloisonne's
dissemination of technological, marketing, and managerial knowledge.
1. Jintan Enamel Arts & Crafts Plant
2. Jintan Baochang Arts & Crafts Plant
3. Baita Metal Arts & Crafts Plant
4. Jintan Education Arts & Crafts Plant

A-02-SHA   Shanghai Guanghui Automatic Instrument Factory
After the implementation of the Spark project, Shanghai Guanghui Automation
Instrument Factory (SGAIF) is able to manufacture small size pneumatic self-regulating
valves with improved quality control and management. The technical support comes from
Shanghai Automation Instrument Research Institute (SAIRI), one of the best in
automation instrument research.
SAIRI had helped to strengthen SGAIF's product design, quality control, and
utilization of information. Together they have shared their knowledge of product design,
process conditions, quality control, and technical management procedures with their
subcontractors who supply mechanical and electrical parts. SGAIF sent engineers and
managers to their suppliers to give on-site support. These measures have helped improve
the technical capacity and quality standards of SGAIF's suppliers. The total sales from
the 12 technology dissemination recipients in 1997 was Y 29 million.

A-07-SHA   Shanghai No. 1 Subsidiary Glove Factory
Under the project the factory updated its PVC glove technology as well as its
production methods. Testing and inspection from an independent institute have shown
that both the quality and the technical specifications of the gloves produced by the new
production line have reached international standards.
